<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer6799285236501637003"><p> Clear-eyed and spirited, Taylor Greer grew up poor in rural Kentucky with the goals of avoiding pregnancy and getting away. But when she heads west with high hopes and a barely functional car, she meets the human condition head-on. By the time Taylor arrives in Tucson, Arizona, she has acquired a completely unexpected child, a three-year-old American Indian girl named Tur</p></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6799285236501637003"><p> Clear-eyed and spirited, Taylor Greer grew up poor in rural Kentucky with the goals of avoiding pregnancy and getting away. But when she heads west with high hopes and a barely functional car, she meets the human condition head-on. By the time Taylor arrives in Tucson, Arizona, she has acquired a completely unexpected child, a three-year-old American Indian girl named Turtle, and must somehow come to terms with both motherhood and the necessity for putting down roots. Hers is a story about love and friendship, abandonment and belonging, and the discovery of surprising resources in apparently empty places. </p><p> Available for the first time in mass-market, this edition of Barbara Kingsolver's bestselling novel, <em>The Bean Trees,</em> will be in stores everywhere in September. With two different but equally handsome covers, this book is a fine addition to your Kingsolver library. </p></span>

        if (this.up('#box')) {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});

          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_706680">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/706680" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/20698-larissa" class="left imgcol" title="Larissa"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1174671588p2/20698.jpg" alt="Larissa" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/706680?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 13, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/20698-larissa" class="user" title="Larissa">Larissa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/329156.The_Bean_Trees" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780061097317" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/20698-larissa?shelf=2007" class="actionLinkLite">2007</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/20698-larissa?shelf=usa" class="actionLinkLite">usa</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/20698-larissa?shelf=southwest" class="actionLinkLite">southwest</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer706680">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9092945475075645008">My stepmother was the type of woman who painted the walls in our house eighteen different colors and wore turquoise-encrusted Kokopelli jewelry to show how in tune she was with the local culture. She hung Frida Khalo prints on the bedroom walls and thought that speaking ‘Food Spanish’ to waiters made her nearly fluent. She also compelled my sister and me to read a lot of Tony Hillerman paperbacks and other ‘local literature,’ which I am now almost positive included The Bean Trees. Because after</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9092945475075645008">My stepmother was the type of woman who painted the walls in our house eighteen different colors and wore turquoise-encrusted Kokopelli jewelry to show how in tune she was with the local culture. She hung Frida Khalo prints on the bedroom walls and thought that speaking ‘Food Spanish’ to waiters made her nearly fluent. She also compelled my sister and me to read a lot of Tony Hillerman paperbacks and other ‘local literature,’ which I am now almost positive included The Bean Trees. Because after reading the first chapter of this book, I got the strangest sense of de ja vu. <br /><br />This is probably appropriate in its way, given that the reason I picked it up in the first place was to suppress a bit of homesickness. Because a couple times a year—amidst the April snowstorms and one too many guys on the subway who splay themselves across two seats while playing audio-enabled Snood on their cell phones—I start pining for the homeland. I turned to this book hoping to get a good dose of Tucsonan flavor to keep me going until I had the time and money to go home and remember why I left in the first place. <br /><br />I have to say, though, <em>The Bean Trees</em> didn’t really do the trick. Because even though I appreciate details about the Sun Tran bus line and the way it smells in the desert when it rains (the thing I miss the most about Tucson), there’s more to invoking a landscape than just listing of things that are really there. A good book about New York, for instance, isn’t good because it mentions the Empire State Building or talks about people taking taxis. It is a major (and frequent) misstep in novels to try and just be factually accurate about a place, without ever getting into how it really feels there. <br /><br />To be fair, though, while the landscape wasn’t terribly reminiscent of Arizona, the writing style really was in its own (probably accidental) way. Because Ms. Kingsolver really illuminates that deep Southwestern flare for ‘characters’ and ‘culture’—a fondness for highlighting how darn quirky desert folk really are, and a gringo’s deep and abiding love of all things latino. <br /><br />(As a side note, though: if we’re going to just start dropping real places into the book for authenticity, I would have swapped the ‘Jesus Is Lord’ tire place for the church that has ‘Happiness is Submission to God’ painted on it—a slogan which often gets altered to ‘Happiness is Submission to Godzilla!’ by persistent neighborhood delinquents…)<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9092945475075645008">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_61138437">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61138437" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/232162-erika" class="left imgcol" title="Erika"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1421895670p2/232162.jpg" alt="Erika" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/61138437?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 11, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/232162-erika" class="user" title="Erika">Erika</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5426778-the-bean-trees" title="Paperback 9780061765223" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer61138437">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17156857146711704166">Marietta Greer has just completed two miracles of her rural Kentucky upbringing: graduating high school and avoiding pregnancy. To celebrate, she jumps in her ’55 Volkswagen bug and rides West, leaving her job at a Kentucky hospital counting platelets to stay true to her plan “to drive out of Pittman County one day and never look back” (11). On the road, she changes her name to Taylor and finds herself in Tucson, Arizona with a broken down car and a Cherokee baby in her arms.<br /><br />Taylor is an honest,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17156857146711704166">Marietta Greer has just completed two miracles of her rural Kentucky upbringing: graduating high school and avoiding pregnancy. To celebrate, she jumps in her ’55 Volkswagen bug and rides West, leaving her job at a Kentucky hospital counting platelets to stay true to her plan “to drive out of Pittman County one day and never look back” (11). On the road, she changes her name to Taylor and finds herself in Tucson, Arizona with a broken down car and a Cherokee baby in her arms.<br /><br />Taylor is an honest, straight-forward protagonist that speaks with youthful tact and an open heart. Through her, Kingsolver voices the morals of an ideal United States brought down with prejudice and misunderstanding. <em>The Bean Trees</em> isn’t a celebration of the Southwest and its adopted mixed-heritage culture as it is a vision into a world stricken by the hypocrisy of that adoption. Comparing her rural Kentucky hometown and Tucson, Arizona together to discover they’re as foreign to each other as to be separate countries, Taylor declares herself an immigrant in her own right and easily warm up to Mattie--the local mechanic--and the plight of the hunted illegal immigrants coming and going from the sanctuary rooms above her garage. She is naive, but warm-hearted, as she struggles to comprehend the idea that a person can not only commit illegal acts, but can <em>be</em> illegal in the eyes of the law, too.<br /><br />When I began the novel, I was not expecting to read about political and human rights issues. I was really surprised to discover Taylor navigating prejudices that are extremely close to home. Now that I’ve finished, I’m blown away with Taylor’s sweet-below-the-surface personality and firm beliefs in the extension of natural human rights to everyone, not just citizens. She finds more in common with Estevan, Esperanza, and Lou Ann Ruiz--her roommate--than she does with the other local folks she meets in town. Themselves displaced from their own points of origin, Taylor and her group form fast friendships and a loyal support system as binding as any family she could ever imagine. Together they help each other survive in a foreign land, everyone as much part rhizobia as part wisteria vine. They are an incongruous family, the titular bean trees, a confusing connotation of the more widely known and beautifully named wisteria vine.<br /><br />Kingsolver’s debut novel is charmingly powerful and subtle in its celebration of families, whatever the form. I’m also entirely prejudiced when it comes to immigration issues and agree with Kingsolver’s politics here (there are a lot of people who will not). I think it’d be difficult to get through this novel coming at it with a closed or contrary mind that would disallow for the suspension of one’s own beliefs. <em>The Bean Trees</em> is filled with rich sentiments that call for an open mind and are impossible to ignore if you want to experience (and enjoy) the book to its fullest.<br /><br />The best part about the book was the dialogue. Taylor and Lou Ann’s colloquial conversations are disarming and honest. It’s very easy to fall in love with their (and everyone else’s) quirks--they bloom from the pages as studies of characteristics we’ve all encountered before; Lou Ann, the worrisome young mother; Virgie, the bigoted senior citizen; Mattie, the bleeding heart. Because of this, <em>The Bean Trees</em> readily comes to life, vividly reminding us of real life issues still very pertinent to our society, even after twenty years. Even little Turtle, who speaks her strange vegetarian language, manages to communicate effectively, if a bit eccentrically, and found in me a sympathetic heart. She speaks a recipe of nourishment, sprinkled here with food, there with a small army of ‘Ma’s determined to raise her right. Like Taylor and Lou Ann finding reprieve in each other’s speech, she finds solace in surrounding her auditory world with comfortable, familiar things.<br /><br />One of my favorites scenes is a complete spoiler, but I think it’s the most powerful in the entire book: emotional and transcendent, reaching far beyond the actions on the page. I’ve dwelt on the political issues, but what drives the narrative are the characters, their personal journeys, and the relationships they form with each other. While it may be difficult to appreciate those aspects without also understanding the politics of what motivates them, it’s hard not to grip the book firm with both hands when Taylor, Turtle, Estevan, and Esperanza sit nervously in Mr. Jonas Wilford Armistead’s office, certain that any sudden movements will break the spell and destroy not one, but four lives. I held my breath and absolutely could not put <em>The Bean Trees</em> down or risk psychologically damaging someone.<br /><br />I had nothing to compare Kingsolver’s writing to. This is the only book of hers I’ve read so I can’t say where on a Kingsolver scale this would land, but I really liked it. What am I talking about? I loved this book. This is the type of literature I think everyone should read and <em>try to understand</em>. It opens a dialogue that I hope engages people in a positive way.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17156857146711704166">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2233966640420173">I quite liked this, though it's obvious that this was Kingsolver's first novel. The main character, Taylor, is unevenly developed--she's too mutable, changing to fit what Kingsolver wants to say or how she wants to say it at various points in the book--and many of the other characters are types, not people, however finely observed. The plotline involving the refugees from Guatemala in particular was a little too anvilicious. And while it's set very definitely in the American South, the novel did</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2233966640420173">I quite liked this, though it's obvious that this was Kingsolver's first novel. The main character, Taylor, is unevenly developed--she's too mutable, changing to fit what Kingsolver wants to say or how she wants to say it at various points in the book--and many of the other characters are types, not people, however finely observed. The plotline involving the refugees from Guatemala in particular was a little too anvilicious. And while it's set very definitely in the American South, the novel didn't seem reminiscent of it--I never really got a picture of Tuscon or Oklahoma in my head--because there was description but no <em>feel</em>. <br /><br />What drew me into this book, though, were the hints of how vivid her writing would become by the time of <em>The Poisonwood Bible</em>: there are some really sharp and oddly beautiful observations, and when she's not trying too hard to drive home a point, her dialogue is nicely observed. Interesting, too, to see that is a book in which men are characterised almost solely by their absence. Enjoyable for the style and the promise, but not for the substance, I think.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4540956607563627220">I have to admit, this book really did a number on me. It was recommended to me from a friend, so my expectations were high, but after the first few chapters I was was not getting into it. The narrator's first-person voice was simple, non-descriptive, and frankly just a bit too naive to handle for an entire novel. But the story was interesting, so I kept going.<br /><br />And the thing is, so does Taylor, the main character. As she charges her way through a haphazard journey to the Southwest, she begins to g</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4540956607563627220">I have to admit, this book really did a number on me. It was recommended to me from a friend, so my expectations were high, but after the first few chapters I was was not getting into it. The narrator's first-person voice was simple, non-descriptive, and frankly just a bit too naive to handle for an entire novel. But the story was interesting, so I kept going.<br /><br />And the thing is, so does Taylor, the main character. As she charges her way through a haphazard journey to the Southwest, she begins to grow up right before your eyes, and so does her narrative voice. Slowly, her language becomes more mature, as do her observations. A story that started out very basic and straightforward becomes rich and multi-faceted. By the end I was shocked at the transformation that happened in just 200+ pages, just as Taylor must have been to see herself and her world change in less than a year. <br /><br />I now have nothing but love for this adorable book. So roll your eyes all you want at the girl in the first few chapters, she'll grow on you.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4540956607563627220">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer8677941004100692937">I love Barbara Kingsolver, but I can't believe this book was ever published. 1988 must have been a slow book year.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText8677941004100692937">I love Barbara Kingsolver, but I can't believe this book was ever published. 1988 must have been a slow book year. I am being generous with the two stars, and I am only giving it that because there were a number of sections which showcased the excellent writer she would go on to become with experience. The characters are all so flat and undeveloped. Taylor makes no sense and was not likable. I never felt that she bonded with Turtle, always saw her as a burden then suddenly at the end, when she was threatened with losing the child she was on a quest to keep her at all cost. If that child was molested as a baby, she would need years of serious professional help and Taylor is clueless. That child would have been better off with Estevan and Esperanza, and for all anybody knows that could actually be their child??? And the storyline about Estevan and Esperanza losing their child but that she was just being cared for by somebody else? That sounds really plausible. And I love how Taylor goes looking for a roommate and finds her new BFF from Tennessee. <br />Oh, and the scene where the Indian woman puts the baby in Taylor's (Marietta's) car and she just calmly sits there watching the woman drive away in the truck. And the dialogue between Marietta and her mother does not sound like authentic dialogue. <br />I guess I really didn't like this book at all. I read it super fast because that's what i do to get through a book i don't like, fast. I know it sounds counterintuitive but hey it's the truth.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_74413620">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74413620" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/670054-jess" class="left imgcol" title="jess"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1229683749p2/670054.jpg" alt="jess" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/74413620?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 13, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/670054-jess" class="user" title="jess">jess</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/30868.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780812474947" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/670054-jess?shelf=2009" class="actionLinkLite">2009</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/670054-jess?shelf=audiobook-d" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook-d</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/670054-jess?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer74413620">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10850582029906287257"><em>"But nothing on this earth is guaranteed, when you get right down to it, you know? I've been thinking about that. About how your kids aren't really YOURS, they're just these people that you try to keep an eye on, and hope you'll all grow up someday to like eachother and still be in one piece. What I mean is, everything you get is really just on loan. Does that make sense?"<br /><br />"Sure,"I said. "Like library books. Sooner or later they've all got to go back into the nightdrop."</em><br /><br /><br />I'm trying to get better a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10850582029906287257"><em>"But nothing on this earth is guaranteed, when you get right down to it, you know? I've been thinking about that. About how your kids aren't really YOURS, they're just these people that you try to keep an eye on, and hope you'll all grow up someday to like eachother and still be in one piece. What I mean is, everything you get is really just on loan. Does that make sense?"<br /><br />"Sure,"I said. "Like library books. Sooner or later they've all got to go back into the nightdrop."</em><br /><br /><br />I'm trying to get better about listening to more audiobooks in the car and less Top 40s best hits of today and your school days. Allow me to be perfectly clear: there is entertainment value in your child knowing all the words to Soulja Boy's romantic serenade "Kiss Me Through the Phone," but it's also rewarding for him to say that Barbara Kingsolver is a good storyteller, discuss immigrants, refugees, and murderous South American regimes on the way home from the bus stop, and groan when the narrator announces the last disc. "There's a sequel! We'll read it! Don't worry," I offered. <br /><br />I picked <em>The Bean Trees</em> to rehabituate myself to the life of an audiobook commuter because I remembered reading another Barbara Kingsolver book in college, and I remembered her writing to be funny and engaging, I remembered she leans toward female protagonists that don't suck, and she wrote that book everyone loves, <em>Animal Vegetable Miracle.</em> I keep meaning to read AVM, but it's got such a long wait at the library. <em>The Bean Trees</em> had no waiting at all, and Sue Monk Kidd said it was one of her all-time favorites it in the Goodreads September newsletter. That's enough good reasons.<br /><br />So I "read" the audiobook of <em>The Bean Trees</em>, and I enjoyed it. The pace of the story is occasionally more of a stroll than a walk, the characters fluctuate in ways that are more convenient for the plot than authentically human, and the dialogue trails off occasionally, leaving the reader hanging. All these things can be annoying, or charming, and I think they work well enough here. So, yes, it reads a little bit like a first novel, which it is. I was quite surprised to realize this was written in 1988 - a number of the sentiments and political views seem timely and contemporary, like Native parental rights &amp; US immigration/refugee policies. This book has feminist characters and stories, it's structured around unconventional families, and includes an emphasis on community support in a way that's not contrived, hokey, or idealistic. Special bonus for the most amazing business name ever: Jesus is Lord Used Tires. <br /><br />The most important things I hope I remember about this book:<br />1. The new year started on July 12, my birthday.<br />2. They spend a lot of time in Oklahoma, which I have done. <br />3. The ladies in this book are smart, independent, and they talk to each other about real life. <br />4. I just love a good, epic road trip with life-altering consequences.<br />5. There's a lady whose "power color" is red, and she wears it all the time. I love people with power colors.<br />6. The theme of unintentional single motherhood &amp; parenting in a fairly unconventional way. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10850582029906287257">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_46705970">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/46705970" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2044409-black-elephants" class="left imgcol" title="Black Elephants"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1234937260p2/2044409.jpg" alt="Black Elephants" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/46705970?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 17, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2044409-black-elephants" class="user" title="Black Elephants">Black Elephants</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/30868.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780812474947" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2044409-black-elephants?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer46705970">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1292480934305569724"><em>The Bean Trees</em> by Barbara Kingsolver is the older twin of a book I read a year ago called <em>Pigs in Heaven.</em> As the first book of the duo, it chronicles the flight of Taylor Greer from a small, hick lifestyle to a freer life she didn't expect. Basically, Taylor's managed to be educated and not get pregnant when she finally takes her car across the country. But one night in a bar, a mysterious Indian woman gives her a young girl. Suddenly, Taylor finds that she's a single mother with no prospects. W</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1292480934305569724"><em>The Bean Trees</em> by Barbara Kingsolver is the older twin of a book I read a year ago called <em>Pigs in Heaven.</em> As the first book of the duo, it chronicles the flight of Taylor Greer from a small, hick lifestyle to a freer life she didn't expect. Basically, Taylor's managed to be educated and not get pregnant when she finally takes her car across the country. But one night in a bar, a mysterious Indian woman gives her a young girl. Suddenly, Taylor finds that she's a single mother with no prospects. What's a girl to do?<br /><br />While writing that summary, I was struck by how interesting the narrative elements seemed. Young, single, white woman faces world with young child that needs love; they triumph. However, I was, to put it bluntly, unimpressed by the entire book. The same thing happened with <em>Pigs in Heaven.</em> I liked the idea Kingsovler presented and I think she has brings real places into fiction, but I just was not impressed by the execution.<br /><br />Another complaint that I had about <em>Pigs in Heaven,</em> which also holds for this book, is that there were moments that made me believe this novel wouldn't age well. Again, I can't remember any particular points, but certain sections would pop out at me while I was reading and make me think.... Hmmmmm....<br /><br />I know Barbara Kingsolver enjoys popularity among literary circles, but I'm really perplexed by it. Compared to the hundreds of other candidates who are more than qualified to be read in classrooms, why choose her?<br /><br />Oh yeah, there were bean trees, and they were a metaphor. But I never thought it worked.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1292480934305569724">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_732630714">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/732630714" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9246169-wanda" class="left imgcol" title="Wanda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1441213786p2/9246169.jpg" alt="Wanda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/732630714?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 12, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9246169-wanda" class="user" title="Wanda">Wanda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/30868.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780812474947" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Wanda by: </span>
      my book club
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=book-club" class="actionLinkLite">book-club</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=female-authors" class="actionLinkLite">female-authors</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2014" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer732630714">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16859955338006751707">This is a character driven novel and if you don’t like the characters, I advise you to set it down, walk away, and read something else. If, however, you are willing to spend a while getting to know the two young women featured, I think you will enjoy The Bean Trees. This is not an action novel—it’s an exploration of the lives of two young women from disadvantaged homes and how they sort out their lives. <br /><br />Who can’t appreciate the desire to get out of Dodge after graduation and see what else the wo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16859955338006751707">This is a character driven novel and if you don’t like the characters, I advise you to set it down, walk away, and read something else. If, however, you are willing to spend a while getting to know the two young women featured, I think you will enjoy The Bean Trees. This is not an action novel—it’s an exploration of the lives of two young women from disadvantaged homes and how they sort out their lives. <br /><br />Who can’t appreciate the desire to get out of Dodge after graduation and see what else the world has to offer? Marietta re-names herself Taylor and truly starts over. She bravely starts out in a hunk-of-junk car and acquires a child along the way. LouAnn takes the more traditional route out—she gets married and moves with a husband, who proceeds to abandon his pregnant wife. But the two young women, from similar backgrounds, find one another and start building a firm friendship. <br /><br />There is a study in contrasts—young women from poor families and illegal immigrants. Taylor, who has felt the weight of discrimination all of her life, is suddenly confronted with her white privilege. LouAnn, who has never felt worthy of anything, is changed by a job where her enthusiasm and hard work are recognized and rewarded. Instead of mooning around, hoping for a transformation of her absent husband, she finally takes charge of her life. Both of them learn new ways to cope with life’s problems and new ways to look at themselves. <br /><br />These are issues that all young women face at some point in life (independence, marriage, careers, children, relations with parents)—how we each deal with them depend on the resources, both financial and friends/family, that we have available to us. I did find Taylor’s ready acceptance of the child, Turtle, to be less than believable. She had finished high school and I thought should have known better than to take off across country with someone else’s child, no matter how abused that child was. And I found the final solution to her legal position to be most unlikely. <br /><br />The significance of the title, which refers to the Wisteria vine, gets rather slapped in your face at the end of the book. The scraggly, ugly vine which, after the life-giving rain, produces luxuriant foliage and beautiful flowers, just as the underprivileged, poor girls flower into a happier life with some kindness from others. Having said that, I loved Turtle’s obsession with plants—wanting to read the seed catalog rather than a story book—even though I can see exactly how it fit into this really obvious message. <br /><br />Despite my perception of flaws, however, I found the book an enjoyable read. It made me appreciate my own age and station in life—I have said it before, I would never choose to be less than 40 again!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16859955338006751707">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_22057733">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22057733" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/796425-jeanette-astute-crabbist" class="left imgcol" title="Jeanette  &quot;Astute Crabbist&quot;"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1304653755p2/796425.jpg" alt="Jeanette  &quot;Astute Crabbist&quot;" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/22057733?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 11, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/796425-jeanette-astute-crabbist" class="user" title="Jeanette  &quot;Astute Crabbist&quot;">Jeanette  "Astute Crabbist"</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/329156.The_Bean_Trees" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780061097317" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-astute-crabbist?shelf=four-star-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">four-star-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-astute-crabbist?shelf=all-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">all-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-astute-crabbist?shelf=america" class="actionLinkLite">america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer22057733">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6904395758458648268">This story was just a ton of fun! I whipped through it very quickly. Nice flow, and at times hilarious, especially in the first half of the book or so. The Southern expressions cracked me up, and I love the way she poked fun at the 80s New Age culture.<br /><br />The style is somewhat similar to Elizabeth Berg, but without so much heavy sentiment. (That's not a criticism of Berg. I like her books a lot, too.)<br /><br />I thought I hated this author because of Poisonwood Bible. I'm delighted to find out she can tell a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6904395758458648268">This story was just a ton of fun! I whipped through it very quickly. Nice flow, and at times hilarious, especially in the first half of the book or so. The Southern expressions cracked me up, and I love the way she poked fun at the 80s New Age culture.<br /><br />The style is somewhat similar to Elizabeth Berg, but without so much heavy sentiment. (That's not a criticism of Berg. I like her books a lot, too.)<br /><br />I thought I hated this author because of Poisonwood Bible. I'm delighted to find out she can tell a great story.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6904395758458648268">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_19696799">

  <a href="/user/show/796950-liz" class="left imgcol" title="Liz"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1327527347p2/796950.jpg" alt="Liz" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/19696799?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 30, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/796950-liz" class="user" title="Liz">Liz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/30868.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780812474947" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/796950-liz?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer19696799'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer19696799">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11617727797207725059">For a large part of this book I had a horrible feeling in the pit of my stomach. I hate reading about child abuse/neglect/human right's abuse. Etc. But, I was feeling a lot better towards the end. The actual ending of the book was a bit disappointing, though. "Steven and Hope" were leading such horrible lives and I had hopes Taylor would give them Turtle. Hopes that the Cherokee Nation would give refuge to Steven, Hope and Turtle. The End. Happily Ever After. <br /><br />The scene in the adoption office was</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11617727797207725059">For a large part of this book I had a horrible feeling in the pit of my stomach. I hate reading about child abuse/neglect/human right's abuse. Etc. But, I was feeling a lot better towards the end. The actual ending of the book was a bit disappointing, though. "Steven and Hope" were leading such horrible lives and I had hopes Taylor would give them Turtle. Hopes that the Cherokee Nation would give refuge to Steven, Hope and Turtle. The End. Happily Ever After. <br /><br />The scene in the adoption office was heartbreaking. <br /><br />At times I felt like this book was originally written as a screenplay. The book would translate well into a movie. <br /><br />I liked Kingsolver's writing. It was gut wrenching at times but, for lack of a better term, "real". As a reader, I could see this happening and see these characters living in Tucson, AZ. There was no glamour, riches or flashy cars within sight of this book. These people lived by the kindness of others and thrift store finds. I have to admit, it's been a long time, if ever, since I've read something like this. <br /><br />For sure, I will read more of Kingsolver's work. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11617727797207725059">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_382885801">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/382885801" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4086118-michael" class="left imgcol" title="Michael"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1439669517p2/4086118.jpg" alt="Michael" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/382885801?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 02, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4086118-michael" class="user" title="Michael">Michael</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/30868.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780812474947" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=racism" class="actionLinkLite">racism</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=immigrant-experience" class="actionLinkLite">immigrant-experience</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=arizona" class="actionLinkLite">arizona</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=native-american" class="actionLinkLite">native-american</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=child-abuse" class="actionLinkLite">child-abuse</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer382885801">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14936928230820974352">In this delightful first novel by Kingsolver, she already has her skills working on all cylinders. The tale portrays a journey of a young woman, Taylor, to escape from a restricted life in a small town in Kentucky. Along the way, an abused 3-year old Cherokee girl is abandoned in her car in Oklahoma, whom she names Turtle, and incorporates into her life at the point her car falls apart in Tuscon, Arizona. With a relatively simple plot and a few characters, she captures well how even poor, uneduc</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14936928230820974352">In this delightful first novel by Kingsolver, she already has her skills working on all cylinders. The tale portrays a journey of a young woman, Taylor, to escape from a restricted life in a small town in Kentucky. Along the way, an abused 3-year old Cherokee girl is abandoned in her car in Oklahoma, whom she names Turtle, and incorporates into her life at the point her car falls apart in Tuscon, Arizona. With a relatively simple plot and a few characters, she captures well how even poor, uneducated people with big hearts can draw in a circle of fellow humans sufficient to handle many tough challenges and to make the essence of a joyful extended family. The impacts of poverty, classism, racism, child abuse, and persecution of political refugees from South America are some of the themes. Despite these subject lines, humor and personal triumphs abound in the telling. Books like this that make me both laugh and cry, as well as encapsulate visions of the universal in the particular, garner highest ratings from me. I place this one in the same ball park with novels of Kent Haruf and Billie Letts.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14936928230820974352">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/1879634-maria" class="user" title="Maria">Maria</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer65565147">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7031245632040918136">This is a well-written novel with a resilient protagonist, beautifully-drawn characters and an inspiring theme of relationships, growth and compassion. It was interesting to me to see this author's progress from this early novel to The Poisonwood Bible, published a few books later, and which is superbly written. In my zeal, I also started Kingsolver's early Animal Dreams, which is thematically somewhat similar but more of a love story, which holds no particular interest for me, but her style is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7031245632040918136">This is a well-written novel with a resilient protagonist, beautifully-drawn characters and an inspiring theme of relationships, growth and compassion. It was interesting to me to see this author's progress from this early novel to The Poisonwood Bible, published a few books later, and which is superbly written. In my zeal, I also started Kingsolver's early Animal Dreams, which is thematically somewhat similar but more of a love story, which holds no particular interest for me, but her style is strong and I'll happily finish it.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_49485340">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49485340" itemprop="url" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/49485340?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 16, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/594637-mary" class="user" title="Mary">Mary</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/30868.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780812474947" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/594637-mary?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer49485340">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9529887863512775922">A decent choice for a quick, well written novel to pass a long airplane ride. Although full of confrontations with "hard issues" like immigration, violence, injustice &amp; single motherhood, these themes weren't given more than an passing once-over. Although easy to fall into and even enjoy, the critical edge and depth that made Poisonwood Bible one of my all-time favorite books was absolutely missing here. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/16930428?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 07, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/263190-jill" class="user" title="Jill">Jill</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/30868.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780812474947" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/263190-jill?shelf=bookclub" class="actionLinkLite">bookclub</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer16930428">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4485064779496849181">I'm a big Kingsolver fan, and this is a great story - probably mostly because it's so original. You're not quite sure how to make out the main character for some time, but Kingsolver has a wonderful ironic way of writing that is highly entertaining. Coupled with the originality, it makes for a great read!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10899718077841933000">So many things about this book bugged me.<br />1. Someone abandons a baby in your car and you don't get ahold of the police.<br />2. Someone abandons a baby, in your broken down car, you don't have a home or money or a destination in mind, so you decide to adopt baby.<br />3. You decide to adopt baby, but you spent the next several years being so bewildered by motherhood that you might as well have left baby in the car to be raised by coyotes.<br />4. Americans in general are directly responsible for the torture of inn</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10899718077841933000">So many things about this book bugged me.<br />1. Someone abandons a baby in your car and you don't get ahold of the police.<br />2. Someone abandons a baby, in your broken down car, you don't have a home or money or a destination in mind, so you decide to adopt baby.<br />3. You decide to adopt baby, but you spent the next several years being so bewildered by motherhood that you might as well have left baby in the car to be raised by coyotes.<br />4. Americans in general are directly responsible for the torture of innocent Guatemalans (in general) because an American manufactured telephone was disassembled and used for electric shock torture by the bad guy Guatemalans. This is your fault ugly American. You must take responsibility for any harm your good inventions cause when used completely out of context.<br />5. Where in the heck does she get her statistics for the random facts she throws in to odd character conversations? 1/4 of all girls are sexually assaulted by a relative? I'm an ER nurse. When you throw out a stat like that you better be ready to quote me your source and it better be peer reviewed.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_827061648">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/827061648" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3769293-elvan" class="left imgcol" title="Elvan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1358709008p2/3769293.jpg" alt="Elvan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/827061648?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 26, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3769293-elvan" class="user" title="Elvan">Elvan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/30868.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780812474947" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer827061648">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12637429536299388431">The Bean Trees is an oddly entertaining and endearing little book. At first I was not sure I could stomach a book that read like a cross between Garrison Keillor's A Prairie Home Companion and something written by Erma Bombeck but the more I read the more invested I became in this quirky little gem.<br /><br />Under the humour and one liners generated by store names like Jesus Is Lord Used Tires, there are themes of separation and loss and finding a family of choice when circumstances prohibit going home ag</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12637429536299388431">The Bean Trees is an oddly entertaining and endearing little book. At first I was not sure I could stomach a book that read like a cross between Garrison Keillor's A Prairie Home Companion and something written by Erma Bombeck but the more I read the more invested I became in this quirky little gem.<br /><br />Under the humour and one liners generated by store names like Jesus Is Lord Used Tires, there are themes of separation and loss and finding a family of choice when circumstances prohibit going home again.<br /><br />I'm glad I didn't give up on this book when it opened with so much silliness. As Kingsolver found her feet in this her first novel, it became quite clear that she has the ability to both enlighten and entertain her readers.<br /><br />Interesting read that may not be everyone's cup of tea. I'll remember 2014 as the year I finally read my first Barbara Kingsolver books.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12637429536299388431">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/38751907?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 27, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/951107-jean" class="user" title="Jean">Jean</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/30868.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780812474947" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer38751907">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13342728509228661521">"...nothing on this earth's guaranteed, when you get right down to it, you know? I've been thinking about that. About how your kids aren't really yours, they're just these people that you try to keep an eye on, and hope you'll all grow up someday to like each other and still be in one piece. What I mean is, everything you ever get is really just on loan. Does that make sense?" I read this book on Thanksgiving morning (when three of my kids were in other states, and one was out with friends), and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13342728509228661521">"...nothing on this earth's guaranteed, when you get right down to it, you know? I've been thinking about that. About how your kids aren't really yours, they're just these people that you try to keep an eye on, and hope you'll all grow up someday to like each other and still be in one piece. What I mean is, everything you ever get is really just on loan. Does that make sense?" I read this book on Thanksgiving morning (when three of my kids were in other states, and one was out with friends), and so it rang a particular chord with me...and the chord was this: I live in a terrible and beautiful world and it is up to me to choose to live with purpose and joy--to bring life to the desert. Thanks for sharing the book with me Gladys (and Michele).</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_34516262">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/34516262" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1283597-michelle" class="left imgcol" title="Michelle"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1302269745p2/1283597.jpg" alt="Michelle" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/34516262?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 10, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1283597-michelle" class="user" title="Michelle">Michelle</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/30868.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780812474947" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer34516262">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17791737426776834619">I just finished reading this book for the second time. I think I did like it better ten years ago when I taught it to my sophomore English class! That is probably because we did activities with the book along the way, and we were able to discuss the book in more detail.<br />Although I think the book is a slow read and it takes a LONG time to get into it, there are some good themes to think about. I do like the ending because it's hopeful and you can see a lot of growth in the characters by the end. <br />T</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17791737426776834619">I just finished reading this book for the second time. I think I did like it better ten years ago when I taught it to my sophomore English class! That is probably because we did activities with the book along the way, and we were able to discuss the book in more detail.<br />Although I think the book is a slow read and it takes a LONG time to get into it, there are some good themes to think about. I do like the ending because it's hopeful and you can see a lot of growth in the characters by the end. <br />The best part about reading The Bean Trees though is that it really got me thinking about my teaching career, and it prompted me to reconnect with a close friend/fellow teacher. We're back in touch now, and that's a great thing!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17791737426776834619">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_801665602">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/801665602" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/318724-chris-blocker" class="left imgcol" title="Chris Blocker"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1352516471p2/318724.jpg" alt="Chris Blocker" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/801665602?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 09, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/318724-chris-blocker" class="user" title="Chris Blocker">Chris Blocker</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/488781.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780060175795" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer801665602">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11109025449299193712">Many who know my taste in literature have, over the years, suggested Barbara Kingsolver to me. Any Kingsolver, they said, you'll love everything she does. Well, the first part of their promise has yet to be seen, but they were definitely wrong about the second. <em>Any</em> Kingsolver won't impress me: <em>The Bean Trees</em> certainly did not.<br /><br />It was probably not the best story to start with. Those who recommended Kingsolver wouldn't steer me wrong—not completely anyway. After all, this was her first novel and it</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11109025449299193712">Many who know my taste in literature have, over the years, suggested Barbara Kingsolver to me. Any Kingsolver, they said, you'll love everything she does. Well, the first part of their promise has yet to be seen, but they were definitely wrong about the second. <em>Any</em> Kingsolver won't impress me: <em>The Bean Trees</em> certainly did not.<br /><br />It was probably not the best story to start with. Those who recommended Kingsolver wouldn't steer me wrong—not completely anyway. After all, this was her first novel and it certainly showed potential. The problem was <em>The Bean Trees</em> was all wrong to me. It reeked of author manipulation. I'm all for the author showing her hand in her work, but it has to be done skillfully—this was not the case here. Kingsolver had an idea—a great idea—for a story, but in order to make it work, she had to bend here and there, align all the pieces on the board just right so that the story made sense. But for me, it didn't make sense. Why would Taylor, who's primarily goal in life is to escape pregnancy, take this child that is thrust upon her with barely a thought? Who said the child wasn't already a victim of kidnapping, the kidnapper quickly trying to rid herself of her crime? The premise is preposterous, yet the story hinges on the reader's acceptance of these facts. Then we sprinkle in a dose of illegal immigration, abuse, and the faulty social work system so that we can get from Point A to Point B, and tie it up with a pretty little bow. It could work, except for the fact it doesn't.<br /><br />I think Kingsolver shows some talent, and if it wasn't the few good scenes she wrote in this novel, along with some good descriptive writing, and for the high recommendations of others, I wouldn't consider returning to her. <em>The Bean Trees</em> reminds me of something Jodi Picoult would've written, and perhaps this is why so many people love this novel. It has that same overworked, syrupy plot. There's certainly a fan base for this style of writing, but I'm not among them. I am sentimental and I love a story that can move me, but this... It's so over processed and reworked and forced into a mold that I could only love it for its potential.<br /><br />So I'll try again. I hear <em>The Poisonwood Bible</em> is fantastic.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11109025449299193712">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10352433893580660877">A delightful first novel by a writer I intend to read in her entirety. From Kentucky (like my wife), the protagonist flees to the hinters into the desert southwest and there the adventure begins. The homey language of this bright young girl and her accumulated child and newfound friends is innocence reborn, soon tarnished by the realities of human cruelty, remains yet <br />unvanquished. This girl and her female friends, and unrequited, hopeless love is told in a time without television, computers or</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10352433893580660877">A delightful first novel by a writer I intend to read in her entirety. From Kentucky (like my wife), the protagonist flees to the hinters into the desert southwest and there the adventure begins. The homey language of this bright young girl and her accumulated child and newfound friends is innocence reborn, soon tarnished by the realities of human cruelty, remains yet <br />unvanquished. This girl and her female friends, and unrequited, hopeless love is told in a time without television, computers or cellphones. It is pure, sweet, and truly original. This author intrigues me, as she tossed this off during a first pregnancy. Interested to see what else she has to teach me, I know I will be entertained, fulfilled and enriched.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer125814025">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1428751877687516459">This was my first Barbara Kingsolver book and I definitely liked it (but didn't love it). The protagonist, Taylor Greer, leaves Kentucky and the near certain fate of barefoot and pregnant behind, only to wind up with an abandoned child left on her metaphorical doorstep (well, in her car, anyway). I loved the relationship between Taylor and the child, Turtle, as well as her relationship with her friend, roommate, quasi-wife, Lou Ann. The pace of everything was a bit plodding for me though and the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1428751877687516459">This was my first Barbara Kingsolver book and I definitely liked it (but didn't love it). The protagonist, Taylor Greer, leaves Kentucky and the near certain fate of barefoot and pregnant behind, only to wind up with an abandoned child left on her metaphorical doorstep (well, in her car, anyway). I loved the relationship between Taylor and the child, Turtle, as well as her relationship with her friend, roommate, quasi-wife, Lou Ann. The pace of everything was a bit plodding for me though and the ending telegraphed from a million miles away. Still and all, an enjoyable read. I am going to try The Poisonwood Bible next.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1330565192">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer908996499863273707">It's funny. I have recently been thinking about women with children living in the same house as best friends, not as sexual lovers, and wondering if it really happens. Well, it happens in this book with Lou Ann and Taylor being roommates in Tuscon, Arizona. Lou Ann had Dwayne Ray and Taylor has Turtle, an abused child who was actually given to her on a cross-country trip. This is a story about moms, moms and daughters, friendship, and what it means to belong. I enjoyed the character Mattie in th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText908996499863273707">It's funny. I have recently been thinking about women with children living in the same house as best friends, not as sexual lovers, and wondering if it really happens. Well, it happens in this book with Lou Ann and Taylor being roommates in Tuscon, Arizona. Lou Ann had Dwayne Ray and Taylor has Turtle, an abused child who was actually given to her on a cross-country trip. This is a story about moms, moms and daughters, friendship, and what it means to belong. I enjoyed the character Mattie in this book. It wasn't my favorite Kingsolver book, and I don't have a desire to read Pigs in Heaven, but I am glad I read this story.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a href="/review/show/1239464623?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 18, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7634363-beth" class="user" title="Beth">Beth</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/30868.The_Bean_Trees" title="Hardcover 9780812474947" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1239464623">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4554220950381051150">A true story of growth! A young girl sets out to find a life of her own away from her small Kentucky town. She has no idea what awaits her, but faces every challenge along the way with grit and determination. She discovers who she is not only in name, but also as a friend, as a mother, as part of a family, and txt importantly who she wants to be for herself. Throughout her journey she is surrounded by a host of wonderful characters that I truly felt like I was getting to know. Many of them grew</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4554220950381051150">A true story of growth! A young girl sets out to find a life of her own away from her small Kentucky town. She has no idea what awaits her, but faces every challenge along the way with grit and determination. She discovers who she is not only in name, but also as a friend, as a mother, as part of a family, and txt importantly who she wants to be for herself. Throughout her journey she is surrounded by a host of wonderful characters that I truly felt like I was getting to know. Many of them grew in ways that they probably never expected because of her and her adopted little girl. Esperanza found some sort of closure and blossomed, Lou Ann grew out of her fear and gained some courage to live life, and Turtle began to grow into a fun, resilient little girl. A great story and a good read.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4554220950381051150">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mWcbcvSKdgxrFb4BRgR+/X/MljJybNeeqVjoE8UP69D8syShBlhi9ufNyro4ZZhPNjtVKzuxkDTDUe2BCZodlQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('v4gW1uMdSKcPHZSPRmcE+gXpY4YTAp/A2jua7mOuqW/aXCkFEc9cXYPF4DQ4BuJITB6gn1rf2GqwMp98rztfKg==')})

Carefully (and also illegally), he tapped into the main irrigation channel. And so began-though few knew it at the time-the Milagro beanfield war. But like everything else in the dirt-poor town of Milagro, it would be a patchwork war, fought more by tactical retreats than by battlefield victories. Gradually, the small farmers and sheepmen begin to rally to Joe\'s beanfield as the symbol of their lost rights and their lost lands. And downstate in the capital, the Anglo water barons and power brokers huddle in urgent conference, intent on destroying that symbol before it destroys their multimillion-dollar land-development schemes. The tale of Milagro\'s rising is wildly comic and lovingly ter, a vivid portrayal of a town that, half-stumbling and partly prodded, gropes its way toward its own stubborn salvation.&lt;\/span&gt;\n Enter Wanda Schultz, a young woman with a broken heart who has come west to search for her wayward boyfriend. Both women are guarding dark secrets and have spent many years building up protective armor against the outside world. This launches a series of unanticipated events, leading Margaret to discover a way to redeem her cursed past, and Wanda to learn the true purpose of her cross-country journey.
